I'd love to see what the current game engine I am building can do with it. I just got the multithreading part of it ironed out. It uses a state controlled thread pool. So depending on the state I can use it in different ways. When in the normal game loop in can run a round robin type system. When initializing and loading files it can use a que task based system. Or I can do hybrid states with some threads doing one and the rest doing the other. I can otherwise control the threads so that when they aren't needed they can be paused and so on. Right now I'm using a z600 works station with 6 cores 12 threads.

Yeah, AMD's EPYC has been shown to be much superior to Intel's current server chips, especially in performance per watt, but apparently a LOT of the software these datacenters run is compiled for Intel and not fully compatible with AMD, so there hasn't been a mass migration. Tom from Moore's Law is Dead talked with some server engineers on his podcast and they said validating the software stack for AMD would take years. That unknown reliability is why they're hesitant to migrate. Just one extended downtime incident because of a program trying to use instructions that don't exist would mean losing big clients.

actually not true this is not the fastest CPU on the planet, it is however the fastest CPU using x86_64 architecture, the real fastest CPU on the planet is known as the Wafer Scale Engine (WSE), made by Cerebras, the original Wafer-scale engine using 14nm technology boasted 1.2 trillion transistors I think. The new WSE-2 now wields 850,000 AI-optimized cores spread out over 46,225 mm2 of silicon packed with 2.6 trillion transistors. Cerebras also revealed that the second-gen chip has 40 GB of on-chip SRAM memory, 20 petabytes of memory bandwidth, and 220 petabits of aggregate fabric bandwidth. The company also revealed that the chip consumes the same 15kW of power as its predecessor but provides twice the performance, which is the benefit of moving to the denser 7nm node from the 16nm used with the previous-gen chip. while yes the WSE is heavily optimized towards AI it's still the fastest CPU on the planet, the thing is the WSE1 in its CS1 package as they sell it was able to beat the Jule supercomputer which is the 61st fastest supercomputer in the world in a combustion simulation, for less than I think half or a quarter of the price.

For whatever it's worth...  I am pretty sure the L3 cache still has a NUMA topology that has variable latency by default, but it's generally faster than the previous generation if the underlying code segments the workload properly.  It's interesting how we're seeing a convergence of the CPU and GPU,  the 3080 TI has about 80 SM's.  If AMD ever decides to introduce an operating mode on their CPU's which streams similar to NVIDIA's SM, these "CPU's" might out-perform "GPU's".

I love how Linus makes out that AMD asking $8k for a processor is alot.... Intel's 28 core Xeon Platinum costs around 3x as much and has less than half the cores, in reality because most server vendors (HPE, Dell, Lenovo etc...) are desperate for end users to buy AMD chips to fund further R&D for future models of AMD rack servers as its only been the last generation of Epyc cpus that enterprises customers have really been sure about buying (prior to this AMD based servers were relegated to non-system critical servers as little trust was placed on AMD chips) as AMD for years haven't had a remotely competative offering to Intel's Xeon lines, however even for single server solutions proper end users would be approaching distributors directly and working with them to pay around half that supposed $8k price tag. (Source : me working in IT distribution for 10 years and designing and selling server solutions with these vendors and chips)

If you run windows 10, even pro, and not enterprise or server (or Linux), then likely the scheduler is slowing down your processor to 64c/64t performance, possibly worse.  The 32 core equivalent can be clocked higher since it has fewer cores to be cooled and they are more likely to be higher performing speed wise.  When you actually buy these, to use the performance they need to be matched with a good OS.

Pop 8 Tesla V100s and you've got yourself a killer Deep Learning box. The way I use my university's 160C/320T compute node is by training multiple independent CNNs and RNNs in parallel. The trick is to be able to automate experiments to fill out a given number of workers, around 20C/40T + 2 GPUs per simulation. I never use the entire thing for one experiment, but the virtualization available using a scheduler lets me light up the entire thing for weeks. I think I process somewhere in the 10s of billions of datum per week.

I have a cool suggestion:  The Einstein Toolkit has a tutorial on how to run a Binary Black Hole simulation: https://einsteintoolkit.org/gallery/bbh/index.html  The simulation in the tutorial was run on 16 Intel(R) Xeon(R) E5-2630 v3 CPUs (128 cores in total) and took about 101 GB of RAM.  It took the cluster about 3 days to finish the simulation.  It would be really cool to see how the 64C/128T Epic processor would do in this test scenario. LTT surely has access to 128 GB of ECC RAM. Personally, I think the Epic wins because in a cluster multiple CPUs have to communicate with each other and lose precious number crunching time. If we are only using one CPU and thus one computational node, the communication time between multiple nodes is gone.  On top of that, we have the large IPC increase of the new AMD processor.  Edit:  Remember the gravitational waves that were first detected by LIGO back in 2016? Well, this is the simulation of that very event (:

The cache changes in Zen3 really helped gaming, but that's a HUGE help in server workloads, where jobs will change from one core to another frequently, and if the data is already in the local l3 cache, it helps a lot.  With the Zen2 architecture, the cache misses when a thread changed cores was fairly frequent, causing more data to be retrieved from main memory, but Zen3 avoids that far more frequently by having more cores per L3 chunk.

To be fair, it's a bit like saying you want to go crabbing in a 12 foot tinny and turning up in the USS Enterprise. Yes, alright, it does have eighty five thousand hp and it can get a 1,100 foot aircraft carrier to about 33 knots. But you're not going to tow it behind your Datto 120Y or launch it single handed or get it into the creek where your pots are. It's huge and impressive but it's completely impossible impractical to use for your normal daily boat. It does very much have its uses, it is very well made and very good at what it does, but it's not a replacement for your old 12 footer. It's a completely different class of animal. Yes, it can play Crysis.... Yes, I could browse the net and keep a watch on my pwn-sites with it, but... 16 cores and 32 threads would do that for me perfectly. Add a 2TB M.2 drive, and it'd be as fast as my network connection would let it be. Put that another way - if I had 50 billion $ in the bank and I could buy Brazil one afternoon for laughs, I still wouldn't buy a top line EPYC to use as a personal computer. I might under those conditions be able to spend $20k on my own personal computing (separate to buying new desktops and laptops and phones and fondle-slabs for about 50 people in my immediate family) but for my own use, a high end laptop and high end desktop and ...

One of the things I'd love to see you cover is a power consumption comparison between this and Intel's offerings, if you can get hold of the hardware to do so. A major reason we're seeing a switch towards AMD in the server market is that density and power are major ongoing costs. The power itself costs money, but it also increases the demand on UPS and thermal management systems, making it a triple-whammy - you know all this already. The density improvement here is obvious, and the power improvement /seems/ obvious in terms of the ratio between the marketed TDPs and the core count, but the practical power consumption of a chip under multi-threaded work loads (e.g. VM hosting, Kubernetes, compilation & CI/CD infrastructure, OpenWhisk and other Lambda-like systems, batch image and document conversion, heavy webapp loads, etc.) is often very different to what you'd see in an all-out all-core load like Cinebench, since they're utilising a far richer mix of computation resources. I suspect Intel still loses here, but I've yet to see a good direct comparison, and it'd be nice to see one. Even some measurements of power consumption on your 16x quad core gaming project would be a good data point to work from, albeit not concretely comparable without a similar test on an Intel platform.
